  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_fb26fb08-643b-489a-a3c8-d3f8f5aaf8bb'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<span id="freeTextContainer18370084264724511601"><b>Sven Hassel's iconic war novel about the Russian Front.</b><br/><br/>'An extraordinary book, which has captured the attention of all of Europe' - <i>New York Times</i><br/><br/>'<i>Legion of the Damned</i> is an incredible picture of totalitarianism, of stupefying injustice...   <span id="freeText18370084264724511601" style="display:none"><b>Sven Hassel's iconic war novel about the Russian Front.</b><br /><br />'An extraordinary book, which has captured the attention of all of Europe' - <i>New York Times</i><br /><br />'<i>Legion of the Damned</i> is an incredible picture of totalitarianism, of stupefying injustice... He is graphic, at times brilliantly so, but never brutal or bitter. He is, too, a first rate storyteller' - <i>Washington Post</i><br /><br />Convicted of deserting the German army, Sven Hassel is sent to a penal regiment on the Russian Front. He and his comrades are regarded as expendable, cannon fodder in the battle against the implacable Red Army. Outnumbered and outgunned, they fight their way across the frozen steppe...<br /><br />This iconic anti-war novel is a testament to the atrocities suffered by the lone soldier in the fight for survival.<br /><br />Sven Hassel's unflinching narrative is based on his own experiences in the German Army. He began writing his first novel, <i>Legion of the Damned</i> in a prisoner of war camp at the end of World War Two.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18370084264724511601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/336693-i-have-a-question-for-hassel-s-readers">I have a question for Hassel&#39;s readers, concerning the Legion of the Damned series. Is it necessary to read the books in order? I don&#39;t have all of them and I&#39;m afraid that would affect the continuity of the story. Is that so? just in case, I possess books 2-3-4-6-8-11.

Thanks!</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_336693'><a id="like_count_community_question_336693" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/336693?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F1058611.Legion_of_the_Damned">like</a></span>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/65073465-sasa-borjanovic"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/65073465-sasa-borjanovic">Sasa Borjanovic</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I would say that it is not necessary to read them in order, there are some connections but each book can be read as separate. The military unit that S<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I would say that it is not necessary to read them in order, there are some connections but each book can be read as separate. The military unit that Sven and his friends were in traveled to many parts of the world during WW2 and each book covers piece of that. I have all the books and read them all like at least 10 times each , but never in order. :) Hope it helps.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_245591579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/245591579" />
    <a title="Luna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4465981-luna"><img alt="Luna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588343893p2/4465981.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/245591579?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luna" href="/user/show/4465981-luna">Luna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer245591579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15826754648399989281">After 100 pages read from this book I've already said to myself that I DO not like this book, because it's too boring even though it's about WW2, which is my obsession. But at some point, the main character, Sven, said that people think that war is someting wow, something romantic and they always expect great stories from it. But the only thing that a war does is destroying people and their humanity and minds. It simply leaves them empty. When I read that I realised that I was thinking the same </span>
  <span id="freeText15826754648399989281" style="display:none">After 100 pages read from this book I've already said to myself that I DO not like this book, because it's too boring even though it's about WW2, which is my obsession. But at some point, the main character, Sven, said that people think that war is someting wow, something romantic and they always expect great stories from it. But the only thing that a war does is destroying people and their humanity and minds. It simply leaves them empty. When I read that I realised that I was thinking the same way and I started to look at the book in a different way. At the end, when I closed it, I was sad and I was about to cry. Soon enough I found out that I was happy when the characters were happy, I was sad when they were having a hard time or when someone died. Barbara's death shocked me the most, even though she wasn't that important. In my opinion, If a book can make me feel this way than it's a good book. What I liked the most was the way in which the author described the minds of those soldiers, their fears and their action. All in all, I loved the way he described the war and the unconsciousness of some people who thought they could win no matter what!<br />It was a good book, and even if it's not real, or it's all lies or whatever all those people say about "Legion of the damned". I still love it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15826754648399989281" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2358965972" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2358965972" />
    <a title="Mr. Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15984059-mr-matt"><img alt="Mr. Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401911574p2/15984059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2358965972?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mr. Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mr. Matt" href="/user/show/15984059-mr-matt">Mr. Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15984059-mr-matt?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15984059-mr-matt?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15984059-mr-matt?shelf=military">military</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15984059-mr-matt?shelf=hf-ww2">hf-ww2</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2358965972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4187242592456991511">War is hell. Sherman said it, but it might just as well have been said by a German tank soldier on the Eastern Front in the Second World War. Partially auto-biographical, and partly historical fiction, Legion of the Damned is the story of one such soldier, Sven.<br/><br/>Sven isn't an eager soldier. He hates the Nazis. He deserts and winds up in a prison camp, but by 1941, the Werhmacht's need for soldiers is so acute that he is released into a prison unit. And then that unit is sent to join the invasion </span>
  <span id="freeText4187242592456991511" style="display:none">War is hell. Sherman said it, but it might just as well have been said by a German tank soldier on the Eastern Front in the Second World War. Partially auto-biographical, and partly historical fiction, Legion of the Damned is the story of one such soldier, Sven.<br /><br />Sven isn't an eager soldier. He hates the Nazis. He deserts and winds up in a prison camp, but by 1941, the Werhmacht's need for soldiers is so acute that he is released into a prison unit. And then that unit is sent to join the invasion of the USSR. Through Sven's eyes we see the horrors of modern, mechanized warfare first hand. We also see the profound wrong-ness of the Nazi regime.<br /><br />The book is entertaining and, at times, very exciting. One of my very favorite sections was the description of the great tank battle at Kursk from the perspective of Sven within his Tiger tank. (As I read I could feel him almost peeing his pants in sheer terror!)<br /><br />The real magic of the book, however, is not in the depiction of the fighting or in the often amusing stories of Sven and his comrades behind the lines. The real magic is in the slow, everyday, callous loss of humanity among Sven and his comrades.<br /><br />The most moving part for me is when Sven and his tank finally kill a particularly hard to kill Russian soldier. Their tank spooks this soldier out of his foxhole. Sven opens up with teh machine gun but misses. Then The Old Un and Stege join in. They even break out the flame thrower. They try and try and try, but no one can get poor Ivan. No one can kill him. It becomes a point of pride. Finally they trap him in a house and set it ablaze, killing the Russian. At that moment, Sven realizes what had happened:<br /><br />"A point of honor. How could I? How could I kill a man just for the sake of my pride? But that is what I did, and I regret it. War with its everlasting murdering and noise and flames and destruction had stealthily poisoned me."<br /><br />By this point of the war (I think late 1943) Sven is a shell of the man that he used to be. The strain and stress are taking a brutal toll. He and his comrades were, in fact, the walking dead. They had lost that spark that makes them vital and human and were just automatons. And by 1944 it was only worse.<br /><br />I think that is the story that Sven wanted to tell with his books. And, as a German, he was uniquely positioned to tell that story. War is hell. It is dehumanizing. There is nothing glamorous or romantic about it. It is dirty and miserable and terrible. War is hell, and we should all remember it. What better legacy for a former Wehrmacht Unteroffizier to leave behind?<br /><br />4 stars out of 5. Great message and at times very exciting to read. It was the perfect book to read on a travel day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4187242592456991511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13878447442612942943">First read this about 25 years ago when I went through a period of reading a bunch of Sven Hassel books. Now they've reissued them I thought I'd read it again.<br/>There seems to be a bit of confusion over how much of his books are autobiographical and how much "based on events" or just plain made up. From what I can gather this is the most autobiographical of the lot though and the first one he wrote.<br/>An antiwar book that shows how he was brutalised and in turn found moments of humanity in the horror</span>
  <span id="freeText13878447442612942943" style="display:none">First read this about 25 years ago when I went through a period of reading a bunch of Sven Hassel books. Now they've reissued them I thought I'd read it again.<br />There seems to be a bit of confusion over how much of his books are autobiographical and how much "based on events" or just plain made up. From what I can gather this is the most autobiographical of the lot though and the first one he wrote.<br />An antiwar book that shows how he was brutalised and in turn found moments of humanity in the horror. Episodic and in some instances frustratingly brief. The horror and futility is balanced by a dark humour, and like most of this type of book a love for his comrades.</span>

<div id="review_425882390" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/425882390" />
    <a title="Alexandra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3443548-alexandra"><img alt="Alexandra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1331009902p2/3443548.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/425882390?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexandra" href="/user/show/3443548-alexandra">Alexandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3443548-alexandra?shelf=autobiography">autobiography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer425882390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2276795421027515128">There is much controversy on the Internet as to whether Sven Hassel's autobiographical books are true or fiction, whether he was what he claims to be - and whether he exists at all or is a fictional identity created by someone else!<br/><br/>Well, it is obvious that the books cannot be taken at 100% face value. However, taking into account that this, the first book was written 12 years before any of the others, and that it appears to cover his entire wartime experience, my impression to say that it is gen</span>
  <span id="freeText2276795421027515128" style="display:none">There is much controversy on the Internet as to whether Sven Hassel's autobiographical books are true or fiction, whether he was what he claims to be - and whether he exists at all or is a fictional identity created by someone else!<br /><br />Well, it is obvious that the books cannot be taken at 100% face value. However, taking into account that this, the first book was written 12 years before any of the others, and that it appears to cover his entire wartime experience, my impression to say that it is genuinely autobiographical (something that emphatically cannot be said for all later books!) <br /><br />The obvious inaccuracies may arise from a desire to protect the identities of those with which he served - it is notable that he only gives biographical details about the deceased. Whether other alterations have been made for more self-serving reasons is open to question...<br /><br />However, the book cover does the contents a disservice. The author never claims to have served in the SS: he deserted from a regular tank regiment, then, after a spell in a concentration camp, served in a penal battalion. He is no Nazi apologist: his tone towards that ideology is uniformly hostile.<br /><br />Whether true or not, this is not a tale of daring exploits; it is about the brutality of war and the suffering it causes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2276795421027515128" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15349688456426420748">I am aware of the controversy that swirls around the author but if even half of this stuff is true (and maybe only half is) it is still mighty disturbing. Despite brief interludes of fleeting happiness, the book is a relentless cascade of brutality: "Such atrocities had a stupefying effect on one. For a long time I went about in a state of queer, dull resignation and was on the point of being infected by my companions' belief that we were all doomed and that nothing mattered at all, since all me</span>
  <span id="freeText15349688456426420748" style="display:none">I am aware of the controversy that swirls around the author but if even half of this stuff is true (and maybe only half is) it is still mighty disturbing. Despite brief interludes of fleeting happiness, the book is a relentless cascade of brutality: "Such atrocities had a stupefying effect on one. For a long time I went about in a state of queer, dull resignation and was on the point of being infected by my companions' belief that we were all doomed and that nothing mattered at all, since all men were evil and sinister without exception."</span>

<div id="review_507978820" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/507978820" />

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/507978820?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon" href="/user/show/6069892-simon">Simon</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer507978820" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15697175544460332093">I'd always avoided these books because the covers put me off, they always seem a bit 'Yay the SS!' Hence my surprise when the book was actually extremely condemnatory of the SS and the whole German regime at the time.<br/><br/>So quite why the book designers decided to put Sven HaSSel emblazoned in bold on the cover I do not know. The guy clearly hated the SS, in fact he recounts with relish the number of times on the Eastern Front that the German army and Russians would stop shooting at each other for a </span>
  <span id="freeText15697175544460332093" style="display:none">I'd always avoided these books because the covers put me off, they always seem a bit 'Yay the SS!' Hence my surprise when the book was actually extremely condemnatory of the SS and the whole German regime at the time.<br /><br />So quite why the book designers decided to put Sven HaSSel emblazoned in bold on the cover I do not know. The guy clearly hated the SS, in fact he recounts with relish the number of times on the Eastern Front that the German army and Russians would stop shooting at each other for a while in order to shoot up SS units that had just arrived on the line, before continuing to shoot at each other.<br /><br />As a novel it didn't quite work, too much jumping around, and the narrative structure itself is weak. But as a snapshot into history I liked it very much. I wonder how auto-biographical it was.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Ian Mapp" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1598886-ian-mapp"><img alt="Ian Mapp" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291393306p2/1598886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1929554387?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian Mapp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian Mapp" href="/user/show/1598886-ian-mapp">Ian Mapp</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1598886-ian-mapp?shelf=war">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1929554387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4787781154959521299">Mrs Mapp, on clocking me reading this, exclaimed "What are you doing reading Sven Hassel? You're not 14 years old".<br/><br/>She was nearly right. I was 13 when I read this.<br/><br/>For a generation of people brought up in the 70s and 80s, Sven Hassel books were de-rigour. Up there with James Herbert. I decided on a trip down memory lane, some 35 years later, to see how they stacked up.<br/><br/>Amazing. When I cannot remember a book read last year, the characters just lept off the page. Porta was always my favourite and so</span>
  <span id="freeText4787781154959521299" style="display:none">Mrs Mapp, on clocking me reading this, exclaimed "What are you doing reading Sven Hassel? You're not 14 years old".<br /><br />She was nearly right. I was 13 when I read this.<br /><br />For a generation of people brought up in the 70s and 80s, Sven Hassel books were de-rigour. Up there with James Herbert. I decided on a trip down memory lane, some 35 years later, to see how they stacked up.<br /><br />Amazing. When I cannot remember a book read last year, the characters just lept off the page. Porta was always my favourite and so it remains - so much more humour than I remember and some great set piece stories of him getting into gambling and womanising scrapes.<br /><br />There's whole passages of the book that I remember too. <br /><br />Once you are past the incessantly grim start of the book, where the narrator is in a penal jail, we move into an episodic tale of the whole war. Future books concentrate on particular events but by the end of this, we have followed Sven through WWII and we know what happens to the main characters in the end.<br /><br />Strong anti-war message and a nice balance between the violence and insanity of war and the little acts of camaraderie and fun that can only happen between people living in extraordinary times.<br /><br />Justified in my adolescent selection. I may even try and track down the next in the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4787781154959521299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1022527621" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1022527621" />
    <a title="Chris Young" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20747219-chris-young"><img alt="Chris Young" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369663200p2/20747219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1022527621?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Young" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Young" href="/user/show/20747219-chris-young">Chris Young</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1022527621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13249936990006188964">The first chapters of this book, when Sven is in the concentration camp, are relentless and harrowing, and are reportedly based on the writer's own experiences.<br/>These beginning chapters get a full 5 stars from me, and a recommendation to absolutely everyone.<br/>So much time has passed now since World War II that we kind of forget just how much of an affront to humanity that these concentration camps were (and still are).<br/>Sven and his fellow prisoners put through their paces, kept constantly on the mov</span>
  <span id="freeText13249936990006188964" style="display:none">The first chapters of this book, when Sven is in the concentration camp, are relentless and harrowing, and are reportedly based on the writer's own experiences.<br />These beginning chapters get a full 5 stars from me, and a recommendation to absolutely everyone.<br />So much time has passed now since World War II that we kind of forget just how much of an affront to humanity that these concentration camps were (and still are).<br />Sven and his fellow prisoners put through their paces, kept constantly on the move with very little time to sleep. Many, many people die. This section of the book leaves you breathless.<br />And then, he is "released" to serve in the army. From here, it seems like a completely different writer has taken over, the tone becomes light. It's like watching "Kelly's Heroes" as Sven and his new comrades get up to all kinds of capers.<br />Like Kubrick's "Full Metal Jacket", "Legion of the Damned" is a story of two halves; the first half being mind-bendingly bleak and the second half being something of a banal let-down, with some exceptions.<br />So the book as a whole gets 3 stars. The brilliant beginning gets 5.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13249936990006188964" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_963759009" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/963759009" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/963759009?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt Hamilton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt Hamilton" href="/user/show/17530993-matt-hamilton">Matt Hamilton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer963759009" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7516200742998256778">This surprised me a little. Having seen the lurid covers, I was expecting a Hassel book to be cartoon tales of battle and Jerry derring do. Was pleasantly surprised to find a thoughtful, more measured approach lurking beneath the whole artifice. I was reminded in many ways of Spike Milligan's war volumes, with the whole idea of war dissected and viewed as hopeless, mundane and forlorn. There was action and battle, but only as background, the vast majority of the novel deals with the sadness and </span>
  <span id="freeText7516200742998256778" style="display:none">This surprised me a little. Having seen the lurid covers, I was expecting a Hassel book to be cartoon tales of battle and Jerry derring do. Was pleasantly surprised to find a thoughtful, more measured approach lurking beneath the whole artifice. I was reminded in many ways of Spike Milligan's war volumes, with the whole idea of war dissected and viewed as hopeless, mundane and forlorn. There was action and battle, but only as background, the vast majority of the novel deals with the sadness and madness of war. Sections reminded me of an updated All Quiet On The Western Front, the German as an unwilling sad part of a huge grinding machine of war. I enjoyed the read and while no classic, a pleasant and interesting work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7516200742998256778" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5692354523631410125" style="display:none">I read everything by Sven Hassel when I was a teenager and, at the time, thought he was great...now, I am not so sure.<br /><br />The Legion of the Damned gives an overview of his time a soldier in the Second World War and goes right through from start to finish. <div id="review_240378825" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/240378825" />
    <a title="Mitchell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3199296-mitchell"><img alt="Mitchell" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/240378825?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mitchell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mitchell" href="/user/show/3199296-mitchell">Mitchell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240378825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16879110101826339698">Sven Hassell wrote a series of mostly fictionalized WWII novels which we would describe as "pulp fiction", from the point of view of cynical, fatalistic German soldiers trapped in the machinery of Hitler's war machine.. This was supposedly his most autobiographical book.. Regardless of the veracity of his writings - which have been hotly contested - (entire series has sales in excess of 50 million), it can't be denied they are ultimately anti-war books.. I find them a guilty good read.. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5979502794210337116" style="display:none">1st - ?? ???? ????! Sto scandagliando il fondo di ogni ricordo onde anobizzare ogni libro possibile, attingo alla vecchia passione liceale per l'odissea di Sven Hassel attraverso l'Europa. Questo � il primo di quattordici, gli altri compariranno se e quando mi ricorder� qualcosa. Questo l'ho riletto. Hassel (cittadino danese) fin� coscritto per varie vicissitudini non esclusa la fame, nell'esercito tedesco. Sfamatosi di cibo ma saziatosi presto di farsi mandare ammazzato, diserta. Mal gliene incoglie. Finisce in una umana tragedia dove le opzioni pi� che le cantiche (inferno / inferno / inferno) sono solo tre. Morte, Lager o Sonderabteilung. I reparti di disciplina, il cui destino � rafforzato - casomai lo scordassero - in una striscetta bianca col jolly roger che portano alla manica. Opta per questi. Il libro � con ogni probabilit� autobiografia sceneggiata. I seguenti - con ogni probabilit� collages di racconti uditi dai reduci uniti sempre meno a materiale proprio. In tutti i casi dove si arriva al grado zero - come qui - c'� solo una cosa che rimane, quel tipo nella buca vicino alla tua, quello che non scapper�. Patroclo c'� sempre. Ma quando non puoi vincere, non puoi pareggiare e nemmeno abbandonare, Ulisse non c'� pi�. Dei miti non ne hanno rimasti punto. La serie fece andar pazzo Bonvi (non � strano, per chi lo conosce un p� che "andasse pazzo" per qualcosa...), che la us� e abus� - da italiano anzi da bolognese per la pi� famosa satira e parodia delle Strumtruppen. In rete si trovano numerose sue foto mentre impersona uno dei personaggi del libro. Mai tradotto in germania, i tedeschi se lo leggevano in inglese. Ah. Hassel ha bloccato per molto tempo il romanzo di guerra, oltre non si va, al massimo si cambia guerra. Sebbene ci siano alcuni punti di contatto e di ispirazione con all'ovest niente di nuovo, qui di studenti ce n'� solo uno, gli altri sono o veri e propri delinquenti o disgraziati o anarchici o gente che semplicemente espose una bandiera rossa durante una manifestazione non autorizzata. A proposito. I libri solitamente ci arrivano prima. I film seguono, e non si fa eccezione anche sulla rappresentazione realistica della guerra. Come tipi umani ricorda "quella sporca dozzina" ma l� si va ancora sulla tragicommedia, Invece, ben prima di "Platoon" molto prima del soldato ryan o della sottile linea rossa, ci era arrivato il grande Peckinpah con l'eccellente "la croce di ferro". Scarso successo di pubblico purtroppo, ma indimenticato per cinefili e fan. Ah, c'� chi si � messo a far critiche asserendo che sui reggimenti di disciplina non si trovano queste vicende. I poveretti scordano che nessun esercito nemmeno i buromaniaci crucchi tenevano registri accurati di tali reggimenti per intuibili motivi. Per cui � ammesso dire che questa roba non la si vuol toccare nemmeno con un bastone, � ammesso dire abbiamo gi� dato, ma scrive panzane no, non si pu�, al massimo si pu� ritenere che l'intera serie "con gli stessi personaggi" sia impossibile. Interessa lo stile? Diaristico, da bollettino o da racconto di taverna. Cosa fosse a spingermi qui non lo so con precisione. Forse, dico forse, il dolce stil novo, e altre piacevolezze inculcate avevano il loro perch�. ___________________________________________ The Legion Of The Damned chapter by chapter: (trama presa dalla rete)------------------- 1. Filthy Deserter Description of Sven's court martial for desertion. Court appearance with Eva Schadows as witness. Sentence to 15 years hard labour. 2. They Died By Day, They Died By Night Long description of Lengries Concentration Camp and reasons for telling story (some serious philosophy here!) Prison food. Executions. 3. Fagen Description of Fagen Concentration Camp. Hard labour. Chance of pardon by disposing of 225 bombs!! 68th bomb turns out to be an aerial torpedo which Sven has to drive out of town on a truck. Hospital experiments, transfer back to Lengries. Sven is pardoned and sent to serve in a penal regiment. 4. One Hundred And Thirty-Five Corpses Description of penal battalion drill and training. More about food. 5. Spit And Polish Penal battalion inspections. Unteroffizier Geerner finds dust in the coffee pot. 6. One Kind Of Soldier The chaplain. Description of Sennelager. Training against tank shock. Swearing of oath. Transfer to tank regiment at Bielefeldt. 7. Our First Meeting "Welcome" by Hauptmann Meier, commander of No5 Company. Sven is assigned to No2 Squadron under Leutnant von Barring, and then to Unteroffizier Beier, leader of No1 Section. Sven reports to Beier and interrupts a card game. Descriptions of Beier, Porta, Eicken and Steyer. The company has a little free time. 8. Curiosities Of The Balkans Porta's sneaky trick on the Nazi railwayman. Food. Railway journey. Halt in Hungarian town of Mak�. Porta steals a couple of geese, gets chased by a crowd of civilians and is rescued by Hinka. 9. The Glories Of The Balkans Discovery of rail freight wagon of food and wine. Porta goes for a gambling session in Bucharest (the origin of his yellow top hat and monocle... he has also won a mistress!) Bit of philosophy from Sven on the occupation of various countries by the Germans. He also criticises Spain where he later went to live. Description of Roumania. 10. Ursula Sven on leave in Vienna to meet Ursula. Train journey. Trouble because of penal uniform. Strange association between a mountain and having sex! 11. The Last Days Description of Ursula. More references to Sven being good at sex. Meeting with Stege's brother. Strange association between the weather and sex! Sven and Ursula get married. Sven is described as an Auslandsdeutscher of Danish-Austrian descent and as a good, naturalised Scandanavian. Return to unit. 12. Porta's Leave Short story from Porta concerning his leave in Berlin and certain accusations made against his good self. 13. Destination North Africa Travel to Naples. Equipment for North Africa. Embark on ship to Tripoli, air raid, shipwreck. Sven and Porta meet in the sea. Porta talks about religion, his adventures in a nunnery and his love of the church organ. Italian plane drops lifeboat and rations. Rescue by Italian Navy. Return to Naples, hunt for Old Un. Transfer to the Eastern Front to be incorporated in the 27th Panzer regiment. 14. Three Girls Old Un gives his philosophy on war. Hans Breuer joins the company. Description of dodges to get out of war. Issue of rations (Porta/rum). Porta and Meier incident, lots of abuse. Unteroffizier Fleischmann helps out. Meeting with 3 girls from KZ and their escape in the armoured train. 15. In Church Visit to church in Pinsk. Porta plays the organ. 16. Before The Attack Hans Breuer gets his foot squashed. 27th Regiment in Smolensk. Old woman pisses in the street. Talk of revenge on Meier. Preparation for action. 17. The Spectacle Guiding tanks in the dark. Preparation for battle. Discussion of battle. Description of battle. Description of Russian winter cold. 18. Captivity Some political discussion and statement on interpretation of writings. Comparison of Hitler and Stalin. Sven and Fleischmann are taken prisoner. Harsh treatment in captivity, escape attempt, train journey East, various adventures in captivity. Discussion of sabotage in p.o.w. factories. Sven and Fleischmann plan to escape. Strange inconsistency as Sven escapes and heads for rendezvous with Fleischmann.... Then Fleischmann is not mentioned again (till Company Commander right at the end of the book). Sven's adventures during escape back to the German lines. 19. The Swine Meier Death of Hans Breuer. Stories of Meier and his brutality. Mine clearing duty. Meier's fate. 20. Sleep Your Fill Boys Rest billet in Russian village. Porta tries to sleep. Big feast at the latrine. Orders to take tanks out onto the plain and dig in. Description of hole digging, inspection by the CO. Discussion of collaboration by Russian civilians. 21. 988th Reserve Battalion Going on leave. Cancelled due to major Russian break through. Fighting at Volkov. First mention of Stalin the Cat. Christmas 1942. Guard duty in no-man's-land. Russians intercept letters from home. Porta disappears. 22. Death Rolls Up Various promotions. Porta is a private at this stage (Stabsgefreiter). Stalin becomes Obergefreiter. April 1943. Completely uncensored letter from Ursula's father explaining her death. Sven's reaction. Tanks battles (presumably this is Kursk - "the greatest tank battle of the war"). More philosophy. 23. Separate Peace Talk of peace and revolution. Move to new positions opposite very peaceful Russians on the River Donetz. Enormous feast. Porta tries to kill an elusive cat. Old Un's dental work on Porta... and Porta's revenge. 24. The Proud Fate The chaplain. 27th Regiment bombed by Stukas. Attack on women soldiers. Retreat. More about the chaplain and his fate at the hands of Sven and Porta. 25. The Armoured Train Looking after recruits. Allocation to armoured train. Sven's nurse friend Asta. Armoured train action. 26. Hals-Und-Beinbruch Armoured car action. Sven and Stege wounded. Journey on hospital train. Time in hospital. 27. Wishing You A Long Illness December 1943. Margaret and Barbara the nurses. Death of Stege - two versions of his death given by the Old Un. Sven leaves hospital to return to unit. 28. The War Continues According To Plan Sven returns to unit which is in bad shape. Sven has to describe his stay in hospital (and the nurses) in great detail to the others. Very depressing chapter!! 29. Soviet Propaganda At The Front Christmas 1943. Russian propaganda and its effects. Film shown in the trenches of food and women. Russian pranks (returning p.o.w.s etc). Story of Russian women's regiment. Von Barring takes to drink. 30. Retreat From Kiev Conflict between Wehrmacht and SS. Front line fighting as infantry. Sven attacks tanks. Graphic description of front-line action. Fetching food through artillery bombardment. Fighting underground in tunnels. Death of Pluto. Covering retreat. Booby traps in trenches. 31. 'There's A Man Lying Out On The Wire' Death of Porta. Sven ends up back in hospital. Short mention of Sven's involvement in the plot against Hitler. Meeting with mad Alpine Obergefreiter. April 1944. 32. Company Commander Death of Barbara. Sven reaches rank of Oberleutnant. Old Un's death and burial. Roll-call of dead friends (including the missing Fleischmann!!) 33. Von Barring Von Barring's madness. Sven's pact with Hinka.</span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9786064306739" href="/book/show/51228254-legiunea-blestema-ilor">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665351-nicole-silverlance?shelf=personal-library">personal-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665351-nicole-silverlance?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3237160876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9561875421585661586">War is hell. War is carnage. The true face of war is depicted in Sven Hassel's Legion of the Damned. <br/>I hadn't read a whole book by him before; I had read snippets here and there from friends carrying these worn out editions that their parents had read before them. I only had to read one chapter to want more. I read one chapter of this when I received it as a gift and it was the only thing I could thing about. This is how good Sven Hassel's writing style is. It sucks you in, holds onto you, but k</span>
  <span id="freeText9561875421585661586" style="display:none">War is hell. War is carnage. The true face of war is depicted in Sven Hassel's Legion of the Damned. <br />I hadn't read a whole book by him before; I had read snippets here and there from friends carrying these worn out editions that their parents had read before them. I only had to read one chapter to want more. I read one chapter of this when I received it as a gift and it was the only thing I could thing about. This is how good Sven Hassel's writing style is. It sucks you in, holds onto you, but knows when to let go of you: when it becomes too much to bear. When too many people die. <br />To my surprise I found myself laughing while reading this book. All the soldiers that Sven befriends in this hell are very drawn, each with their own faith and distinct personality, each bringing their own light in this hell hole, but at the same time they all think alike: they want the war to be over, they want to go home and they each pray that the other manages to go home. They never pray for themselves.<br />The soldiers in this book are sent to fight the Russians and if you have the smallest amount of knowledge about how that went, you should know how this book will go. The level of detail is still shocking, stirs fear into the reader's heart and sometimes you find yourself weeping whenever a soldier falls (actually, when a soldier is obliterated). <br />Even now, after I have finished the book I still find myself staring at the cover, looking at the skeletal face of this nazi soldier playing the violin and cannot help but imagine him doing just that as soldiers scream in agony around him, as bombs go off and bullets fly near his skull. It's my euphoric image of hell, the image Sven Hassel pushed into my brain, the true face of war. War stories must not be told by those sitting behind desks and sending people to the slaughterhouse. War stories must be written by soldiers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9561875421585661586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1881660289095010405"> I think that this is first war book that I have ever read, when I was nearly 17. It made huge impact especially final lines, were the author marches his dead comrades in front the generals and politicans who pushed them into the first lines.<br/>Very soon there was a civil war in part of our country. On the one hand like every silly juvenile you want to rush into battle, but as I have already read 5 books off mine late father`s collection I was easily cured off romantic side of any combat.<br/>With so ma</span>
  <span id="freeText1881660289095010405" style="display:none"> I think that this is first war book that I have ever read, when I was nearly 17. It made huge impact especially final lines, were the author marches his dead comrades in front the generals and politicans who pushed them into the first lines.<br />Very soon there was a civil war in part of our country. On the one hand like every silly juvenile you want to rush into battle, but as I have already read 5 books off mine late father`s collection I was easily cured off romantic side of any combat.<br />With so many books written about wars how easily people are tricked to enter it is a puzzle for me still.<br />This kind of book should should be use as lecture for many.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25687987-oliver-eike?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25687987-oliver-eike?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25687987-oliver-eike?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25687987-oliver-eike?shelf=series">series</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer940697852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12887219382458195647">At first as i started to read this i did not know it was an autobiography, the book started out good, but when it hit dialogue heavy "romantic" parts, to call it boring would be being kind. For it was painful to read. The dialogues were so stale and akward that it felt unnatural. <br/><br/>But as it dawned on me, just what it was i was reading, that it was indeed an autobiography, as i rememberd that the book itself was written while Sven Hassel sat in a Prisoner of War prison for treachery against the Ge</span>
  <span id="freeText12887219382458195647" style="display:none">At first as i started to read this i did not know it was an autobiography, the book started out good, but when it hit dialogue heavy "romantic" parts, to call it boring would be being kind. For it was painful to read. The dialogues were so stale and akward that it felt unnatural. <br /><br />But as it dawned on me, just what it was i was reading, that it was indeed an autobiography, as i rememberd that the book itself was written while Sven Hassel sat in a Prisoner of War prison for treachery against the German state, by a soldier who had fought with the penal legions in most fronts across the 2nd World War... <br /><br />Of course a man so scarred by war would struggle with things most people see as normal, to him the combat scenes came as natural as writing things on Facebook is to people of this generation. And therein lays the great sadness of this book. <br /><br />You get a harsh look at war, written by a man who grew to hate the very notion of it, he despiced what war made mankind into, what it could do to a humans mind, how it can poison us all if we let it. <br /><br />There are comical and romantic moments, there are simply lewd and odd moments, but it is all there as contrasts, for if all one see is death, one would need to let the crazy as well as the warmth of another in to not go insane from what one was forced to do. <br /><br />So do not see the boring parts of the book as just that as i did at first, see them more as food for his soul, what he needed to let the world still have meaning to him. <br /><br />All in all, a very good book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12887219382458195647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1726558551" />
    <a title="Dragos Iosif" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36435054-dragos-iosif"><img alt="Dragos Iosif" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1726558551?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dragos Iosif" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dragos Iosif" href="/user/show/36435054-dragos-iosif">Dragos Iosif</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36435054-dragos-iosif?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1726558551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12328490999932566885">This book is very uneven. Judging by the style and events depicted, I would attribute this work to multiple authors trying to write multiple types of books. And the editor did not make a good job on blending all these things into something more organic. From one chapter to the other, without any notice, we go from the absolute horrors of war/imprisonment to the soldiers' various shenanigans. <br/><br/>And while this can be understandable and can provide some insight into a soldier's mind, the immersion is</span>
  <span id="freeText12328490999932566885" style="display:none">This book is very uneven. Judging by the style and events depicted, I would attribute this work to multiple authors trying to write multiple types of books. And the editor did not make a good job on blending all these things into something more organic. From one chapter to the other, without any notice, we go from the absolute horrors of war/imprisonment to the soldiers' various shenanigans. <br /><br />And while this can be understandable and can provide some insight into a soldier's mind, the immersion is fully broken by the narrator's attempt to force feed you with morality. The actions depicted offer enough information for a reader to form his own opinion on the matter of war. They do their job well in that manner. But then the narrator starts putting his own words into his characters' mouths and, as if it weren't enough, he's often adressing his thoughts directly to the reader. This takes so much away from the whole experience and makes the book very verbose at times.<br /><br />What is more, I sometimes got the feeling that Hassel was a bit of an asshole who modified the reality to suit his needs("Hey guys, I really never liked these Nazi anyway. I was secretly always on your side." riiiiiiight) or the needs of the book("Well, I'm almost at the end of the story and I totally forgot to add some action. OK. Tank battles it is. Oh and nobody of importance died around me? That's odd. Better take care of it now.").<br /><br />All in all, it is an enjoyable book, easy to read, manages to represent a lot of the various aspects of war, but I would not go as far as consider it autobiographical.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12328490999932566885" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11133988-legion-of-the-damned">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1645756853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9819295446781273004">This first of the 14 books in this series is more focused, darker, and intense than the 3rd (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144345.Comrades_of_War__Cassell_Military_Paperbacks_' title='Comrades of War (Cassell Military Paperbacks) by Sven Hassel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Comrades of War</a>) which I reviewed recently. Probably Hassel thought this could be the only book. It also covers more ground, 3 years from 1941 to 1944.<br/><br/>It ends with a litany of the lost, a visit to an asylum, and a promise.<br/><br/>It is compulsively readable. Hassel skilfully treats many aspects of life in those dark times: tank warfare, concentration camps, love, women, comradeship, officers.<br/><br/>Now I know I'll read</span>
  <span id="freeText9819295446781273004" style="display:none">This first of the 14 books in this series is more focused, darker, and intense than the 3rd (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144345.Comrades_of_War__Cassell_Military_Paperbacks_" title="Comrades of War (Cassell Military Paperbacks) by Sven Hassel" rel="nofollow noopener">Comrades of War</a>) which I reviewed recently. Probably Hassel thought this could be the only book. It also covers more ground, 3 years from 1941 to 1944.<br /><br />It ends with a litany of the lost, a visit to an asylum, and a promise.<br /><br />It is compulsively readable. Hassel skilfully treats many aspects of life in those dark times: tank warfare, concentration camps, love, women, comradeship, officers.<br /><br />Now I know I'll read the remaining 12 books too. I can take the feverish intensity and nightmare images only because everything Hassel describes happened to real people, day in and day out for 6 years. And I know they still happen to this very day: if you can read without blanching descriptions of how North Korea treats victims in its secret camps, you have a stronger stomach than me.<br /><br />Actually there is another reason I can read these books, even with great enjoyment: as I said the books are readable in the highest degree. The reason is that the books are so lively. Despite the gruesome foreground, a sense of life pulses yet more strongly in the background.</span>

A great anti war Story.<br/><br/>I was reluctant to buy this book in the first place, as the cover designs of many other Versions gave me the Impression that this is some Kind of nazi Propaganda book.<br/><br/>But this is as far from the truth as possible.<br/><br/>We follow the Story of Sven who is fighting with his comrades on different fronts in WWII - main Focus is not on the description of various battle Scenes but the intense emotions, Motivation and deep thoughts of the soldiers.<br/><br/>More than onc</span>
  <span id="freeText9122247758975095559" style="display:none">This is a anti-war Story. A great anti war Story.<br /><br />I was reluctant to buy this book in the first place, as the cover designs of many other Versions gave me the Impression that this is some Kind of nazi Propaganda book.<br /><br />But this is as far from the truth as possible.<br /><br />We follow the Story of Sven who is fighting with his comrades on different fronts in WWII - main Focus is not on the description of various battle Scenes but the intense emotions, Motivation and deep thoughts of the soldiers.<br /><br />More than once the author directly condemns war in general and of course specifically WWII and the different aspects of it.<br /><br />I read many war Accounts - Vietnam veterans, WWII, WWI, Korea and many more. <br />Comparing the stories reveals so many similarities in the emotional state of the fighters that it is safe to say that even if Sven Hassel is recounting tales of others, it is done very well. <br />I personally do believe that Sven Hassel did really experience most of the Accounts himself. <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_895944928" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/895944928" />
    <a title="Hamish Davidson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13816232-hamish-davidson"><img alt="Hamish Davidson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578048754p2/13816232.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/895944928?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hamish Davidson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hamish Davidson" href="/user/show/13816232-hamish-davidson">Hamish Davidson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13816232-hamish-davidson?shelf=eastern-europe">eastern-europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer895944928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6099461947465762639">Initial illusions proved wrong: Upon starting this book, I thought the translation from 1950s German to English seemed a bit wooden, but after 70 pages I was really involved. Sven also has a great sense of humour. Before starting this book, I didn't know if would resonate with me as well as it did with the millions of readers who came two generations before me (nor were the 1980s covers I saw growing up appealing to me). I was wrong again, in the best possible way. Young people these days don't </span>
  <span id="freeText6099461947465762639" style="display:none">Initial illusions proved wrong: Upon starting this book, I thought the translation from 1950s German to English seemed a bit wooden, but after 70 pages I was really involved. Sven also has a great sense of humour. Before starting this book, I didn't know if would resonate with me as well as it did with the millions of readers who came two generations before me (nor were the 1980s covers I saw growing up appealing to me). I was wrong again, in the best possible way. Young people these days don't speak nearly as colourfully or creatively as the boy soldiers of World War Two. I got a real kick out of this book.<br /><br />The characters are strong, and real. The story is told in a way that you are frequently caught by surprise, as when watching a well crafted movie. I wish I didn't wait so long to begin reading Sven Hassel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6099461947465762639" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1785931174?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Huw Collingbourne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Huw Collingbourne" href="/user/show/7620322-huw-collingbourne">Huw Collingbourne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1785931174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12988007354502008496">This was considerably better than I anticipated. The Sven Hassel books were very popular in Britain in the early '70s. They all had very lurid covers with the SS of Hassel shown in the German SS font. I assumed these books would be all violence and derring-do. While there is quite a bit of that, there is also much humour (some of it almost Wodehousian) and serious, sometimes disturbing descriptions of life and warfare under the Nazi regime. I know that there is debate about just how much of Hass</span>
  <span id="freeText12988007354502008496" style="display:none">This was considerably better than I anticipated. The Sven Hassel books were very popular in Britain in the early '70s. They all had very lurid covers with the SS of Hassel shown in the German SS font. I assumed these books would be all violence and derring-do. While there is quite a bit of that, there is also much humour (some of it almost Wodehousian) and serious, sometimes disturbing descriptions of life and warfare under the Nazi regime. I know that there is debate about just how much of Hassel's first-person narratives are really based on his own experiences. All I can say is that in this book at least I got the distinct impression that when reality didn't meet the needs of drama, Hassel never felt any compunction about adding whatever was necessary to move the story along. Ultimately it felt a bit like the World War 2 exploits of Flashman. Which may be a compliment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12988007354502008496" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NNNfa1wethKjad/p6yY+oroI/TIHTinEukkOKZxSgW4TNcPv/QTeUGbyn/SBYdSeFuOTTEEPXMXAbDAuSWw2Qw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Traiul populaţiei din Siliştea-Gumeşti nu e uşor deloc, decât pentru câteva familii înstărite, familiile unora ca alde Aristide, Cotelici, Bălosu sau Iocan, dar nici peste măsură de amărât nu este. Hotarul comunei cuprinde, loturi mai vaste sau mai restrânse ale ţăranilor şi moşia Marica, vegheată cu străşnicie de un paznic. Afară de bogătaşii satului, care au case mari, ţăranii ceilalţi vieţuiesc în case cu două sau trei camere şi chiar in bordeie.\nGospodăria Moromeţilor pare solidă şi grija conducătorului ei este s-o menţină intactă. Davidescu, M. Ralea), romanul Ciuleandra (1927) nu a fost creditat ca o narațiune de performanță, la nivelul capodope<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16662099998302392497\" style=\"display:none\">Deși a fost în general bine primit imediat după apariție de critica de întâmpinare în exercițiu (au scris favorabil Perpessicius, N. Davidescu, M. Ralea), romanul Ciuleandra (1927) nu a fost creditat ca o narațiune de performanță, la nivelul capodoperelor unui scriitor consacrat prin Ion (1920) și Pădurea spânzuraților (1922). Criticii (în mod special Pompiliu Constantinescu) i-au reproșat fie superficialitatea analizei psihologice, fie intriga senzațională, fie artificiozitatea conflictului melodramatic. Autorul însuși a mărturisit în jurnal ușurința cu care a scris micul roman într-o perioadă de tatonare și mobilizare a eforturilor de construcție pentru Răscoala. Transcrierea finală a romanului, în august 1927, a avut totuși dificultățile ei, datorită îndoielilor autorului, suspiciunii că narațiunea suferă de artificialitate: &quot;Parcă e ceva livresc în viața pe care o trăiesc figurile&quot;-notează scriitorul în jurnal la data de 8 august 1927 (v. Opere 17, p. 6). Printr-o excepție de la credința rebreniană statornică în arta majoră serioasă, animată de o semnificație morală clară, Ciuleandra &quot;ar fi o demonstrație a artei privită ca simplu joc: nu vrea să propovăduiască și nici să-ți lase vreun învățământ. Nu vrea decât să amuze în sensul larg al cuvântului&quot;(ibidem, p. 6). Autorul nu-și abandonează însă romanul sub umbrela nesatisfăcătoare a ideii de joc, adică de &quot;operă de artă prea pură sau prea sintetică&quot;, pentru că l-ar destina astfel categoriei minore de simple &quot;experiențe de laborator&quot;. Liviu Rebreanu își explicitează în jurnal adevărata investiție de semnificație: &quot;Ciuleandra asta, pentru mine e o operă în care se exprimă și se clarifică o taină sufletească mare, e cazul, des repetat, al iubirii până la crimă...&quot;(Opere 17, p. 7). Orgolios, după apariția cărții nu agrea opiniile critice care nu o tratau cu încredere și cu suficientă seriozitate.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16662099998302392497\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/83447.Sven_Hassel">About Sven Hassel</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<a href="/author/show/83447.Sven_Hassel"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1313055094p3/83447.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16177870539196949805">Hassel served in the Danish merchant navy till 1937, when he moved to Germany to join the army. He served with the second Panzer Division stationed at Eisenach and in 1939 was a tank driver during the invasion of Poland. A year later he attempted to escape because of being mentally exhausted. He was transferred to a Sonderabteilung, a penal unit manned by criminals and dissidents. He served with t</span>
  <span id="freeText16177870539196949805" style="display:none">Hassel served in the Danish merchant navy till 1937, when he moved to Germany to join the army. He served with the second Panzer Division stationed at Eisenach and in 1939 was a tank driver during the invasion of Poland. A year later he attempted to escape because of being mentally exhausted. He was transferred to a Sonderabteilung, a penal unit manned by criminals and dissidents. He served with the 2nd Cavalry Regiment and later the 11th and 27th Panzer Regiments (6th Panzer Division) on all fronts except North Africa and was wounded several times. Eventually he reached the rank of lieutenant and received an Iron Cross 1st and 2nd class. He surrendered to Soviet troops in Berlin in 1945 and spent the following years in various POW camps. He began to write his first book <i>Legion of the Damned</i> while he was interned. He was released in 1949, and was planning to join the French Foreign Legion when he met Dorthe Jensen. They got married in 1951. He went to work in a car factory. In 1957 Sven Hassel suffered from an attack of a sickness caught during the war and was paralyzed for almost two years. After recovery, he began to write more books. <br /><br />See also <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7136169.Sven_Hazel" title="Sven Hazel" rel="nofollow noopener">Sven Hazel</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="16177870539196949805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1045184-de-ford-mtes-legion">Quotes from Legion of the Damned</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Nobody believes that – or, rather, nobody dares believe it. What is this curse that makes us all so inert and cowed that we cannot pull ourselves together and deprive the generals of their grants? We are oafs most of us, lazy and ignorant, and we nod our heads and are satisfied when we hear a lot of profound nonsense about ‘balance of power’ and ‘interplay of forces’ and all the rest of the jargon. Balance of power? If every Tom, Dick and Harry were to exert himself and demand that the money should be spent on making him better off instead of on arms and war there would be no war and we should all be better off. But the Toms, Dicks and Harrys must first bang their fists on the table and let it be known who holds the power and how that power is to be used.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
